</li>
</ul>
</center>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bases">Bases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Bases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Navy_installations" title="List of United States Navy installations">List of United States Navy installations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_Navy_bases.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/United_States_Navy_bases.svg/300px-United_States_Navy_bases.svg.png" width="300" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/United_States_Navy_bases.svg/450px-United_States_Navy_bases.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/United_States_Navy_bases.svg/600px-United_States_Navy_bases.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="959" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_Navy_bases.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Navy bases in the United States</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The size, complexity, and international presence of the United States Navy requires a large number of navy installations to support its operations. While the majority of bases are located inside the United States itself, the navy maintains a significant number of facilities abroad, either in U.S.-controlled territories or in foreign countries under a <a href="/wiki/Status_of_Forces_Agreement" title="Status of Forces Agreement" class="mw-redirect">Status of Forces Agreement</a> (SOFA).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_United_States">Eastern United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Eastern United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The second largest concentration of installations is at <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Roads" title="Hampton Roads">Hampton Roads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, where the navy occupies over 36,000 acres (15,000&#160;ha) of land. Located at Hampton Roads are <a href="/wiki/NS_Norfolk" title="NS Norfolk" class="mw-redirect">Naval Station Norfolk</a>, homeport of the Atlantic Fleet; <a href="/wiki/NAS_Oceana" title="NAS Oceana" class="mw-redirect">NAS Oceana</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Master_Jet_Base" title="Master Jet Base" class="mw-redirect">Master Jet Base</a>; <a href="/wiki/Naval_Amphibious_Base_Little_Creek" title="Naval Amphibious Base Little Creek">Naval Amphibious Base Little Creek</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Training_Support_Center_Hampton_Roads" title="Training Support Center Hampton Roads">Training Support Center Hampton Roads</a> as well as a number of Navy and commercial shipyards that service navy vessels. Also in Virginia is the Aegis Training and Readiness Center located at NSA Dahlgren. Maryland is home to <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Patuxent_River" title="Naval Air Station Patuxent River">Naval Air Station Patuxent River</a>, which houses the Navy's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Test_Pilot_School" title="United States Naval Test Pilot School">Test Pilot School</a>. Also located in Maryland is the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">United States Naval Academy</a>, situated in <a href="/wiki/Annapolis" title="Annapolis" class="mw-redirect">Annapolis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Naval_Station_Newport" title="Naval Station Newport">Naval Station Newport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a> is home to many schools and tenant commands, including the <a href="/wiki/Officer_Candidate_School" title="Officer Candidate School">Officer Candidate School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naval_Undersea_Warfare_Center" title="Naval Undersea Warfare Center">Naval Undersea Warfare Center</a>, and more, and also maintains inactive ships.</p>
<p>There is also a naval base in Charleston, South Carolina. This is home to the Nuclear A-School, and the Nuclear Field Power school, and one of two nuclear 'Prototype' Schools. The state of Florida is the location of three major bases, <a href="/wiki/Naval_Station_Mayport" title="Naval Station Mayport">Naval Station Mayport</a>, the Navy's fourth largest, in <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville, Florida</a>; <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Jacksonville" title="Naval Air Station Jacksonville">Naval Air Station Jacksonville</a>, a Master Air Anti-submarine Warfare base; and <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Pensacola" title="Naval Air Station Pensacola">Naval Air Station Pensacola</a>; home of the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Education_and_Training_Command" title="Naval Education and Training Command">Naval Education and Training Command</a>, the Naval Air Technical Training Center that provides specialty training for enlisted aviation personnel and is the primary flight training base for Navy and Marine Corps <a href="/wiki/Naval_Flight_Officer" title="Naval Flight Officer" class="mw-redirect">Naval Flight Officers</a> and enlisted <a href="/wiki/Naval_Aircrewman" title="Naval Aircrewman" class="mw-redirect">Naval Aircrewmen</a>. There is also Naval Support Activity in Panama City Beach, Florida which is home to the Navy Diving and Salvage Training Center.</p>
<p>The main U.S. Navy <a href="/wiki/Submarine_base" title="Submarine base">submarine bases</a> on the east coast are located in <a href="/wiki/Groton,_Connecticut" title="Groton, Connecticut">Groton, Connecticut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kings_Bay,_Georgia" title="Kings Bay, Georgia">Kings Bay, Georgia</a>. There is a naval shipyard in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth,_New_Hampshire" title="Portsmouth, New Hampshire">Portsmouth, New Hampshire</a>, which repairs naval submarines.<sup id="cite_ref-status_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-status-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Naval_Station_Great_Lakes" title="Naval Station Great Lakes">Naval Station Great Lakes</a>, north of Chicago, Illinois is the home of the Navy's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Recruit_Training" title="United States Navy Recruit Training" class="mw-redirect">boot camp</a> for enlisted sailors.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_United_States_and_Hawaii">Western United States and Hawaii</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Western United States and Hawaii">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_SEALs_SEAL_jumps_over_side_boat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/US_Navy_SEALs_SEAL_jumps_over_side_boat.jpg/220px-US_Navy_SEALs_SEAL_jumps_over_side_boat.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/US_Navy_SEALs_SEAL_jumps_over_side_boat.jpg/330px-US_Navy_SEALs_SEAL_jumps_over_side_boat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/US_Navy_SEALs_SEAL_jumps_over_side_boat.jpg/440px-US_Navy_SEALs_SEAL_jumps_over_side_boat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="330" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_SEALs_SEAL_jumps_over_side_boat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Underwater_Demolition_Team" title="Underwater Demolition Team">Underwater Demolition Team</a> members using the casting technique from a speeding boat.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_120209-N-XD935-302_Mass_Communication_Specialist_1st_Class_Shane_Tuck,_assigned_to_the_Expeditionary_Combat_Camera_Underwater_Photo_Team,_c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/US_Navy_120209-N-XD935-302_Mass_Communication_Specialist_1st_Class_Shane_Tuck%2C_assigned_to_the_Expeditionary_Combat_Camera_Underwater_Photo_Team%2C_c.jpg/220px-US_Navy_120209-N-XD935-302_Mass_Communication_Specialist_1st_Class_Shane_Tuck%2C_assigned_to_the_Expeditionary_Combat_Camera_Underwater_Photo_Team%2C_c.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/US_Navy_120209-N-XD935-302_Mass_Communication_Specialist_1st_Class_Shane_Tuck%2C_assigned_to_the_Expeditionary_Combat_Camera_Underwater_Photo_Team%2C_c.jpg/330px-US_Navy_120209-N-XD935-302_Mass_Communication_Specialist_1st_Class_Shane_Tuck%2C_assigned_to_the_Expeditionary_Combat_Camera_Underwater_Photo_Team%2C_c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/US_Navy_120209-N-XD935-302_Mass_Communication_Specialist_1st_Class_Shane_Tuck%2C_assigned_to_the_Expeditionary_Combat_Camera_Underwater_Photo_Team%2C_c.jpg/440px-US_Navy_120209-N-XD935-302_Mass_Communication_Specialist_1st_Class_Shane_Tuck%2C_assigned_to_the_Expeditionary_Combat_Camera_Underwater_Photo_Team%2C_c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2784" data-file-height="1848" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_120209-N-XD935-302_Mass_Communication_Specialist_1st_Class_Shane_Tuck,_assigned_to_the_Expeditionary_Combat_Camera_Underwater_Photo_Team,_c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Combat Camera Underwater Photo Team – A US Navy diver during <a href="/wiki/Underwater_photography" title="Underwater photography">underwater photography</a> training off the coast of Guantanamo Bay.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The navy's largest complex is <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Weapons_Station_China_Lake" title="Naval Air Weapons Station China Lake">China Lake</a>, California, which covers 1.1&#160;million acres (4,500&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of land, or approximately 1/3 of the United States Navy's total land holdings.<sup id="cite_ref-status_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-status-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Naval_Base_San_Diego" title="Naval Base San Diego">Naval Base San Diego</a>, California, is the main homeport of the Pacific Fleet (although its headquarters is located in <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a>, Hawaii). <a href="/wiki/NAS_North_Island" title="NAS North Island" class="mw-redirect">NAS North Island</a> is located on the north side of <a href="/wiki/Coronado,_California" title="Coronado, California">Coronado</a>, and is home to Headquarters for Naval Air Forces and Naval Air Force Pacific, the bulk of the Pacific Fleet's helicopter squadrons, and part of the West Coast <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a> fleet. <a href="/wiki/Naval_Amphibious_Base_Coronado" title="Naval Amphibious Base Coronado">Naval Amphibious Base Coronado</a> is located on the southern end of the Coronado Island and is home to the navy's west coast SEAL teams and special boat units. Naval Amphibious Base Coronado is also home to the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Special_Warfare_Center" title="Naval Special Warfare Center">Naval Special Warfare Center</a>, the primary training center for SEALs.</p>
<p>The other major collection of naval bases on the west coast is in <a href="/wiki/Puget_Sound" title="Puget Sound">Puget Sound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(U.S._state)" title="Washington (U.S. state)" class="mw-redirect">Washington</a>. Among them, <a href="/wiki/Naval_Station_Everett" title="Naval Station Everett">Naval Station Everett</a> is one of the newer bases and the navy states that it is its most modern facility.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/NAS_Fallon" title="NAS Fallon" class="mw-redirect">NAS Fallon</a>, Nevada serves as the primary training ground for navy strike aircrews, and is home to the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Strike_Air_Warfare_Center" title="Naval Strike Air Warfare Center" class="mw-redirect">Naval Strike Air Warfare Center</a>. <a href="/wiki/Master_Jet_Base" title="Master Jet Base" class="mw-redirect">Master Jet Bases</a> are also located at <a href="/wiki/NAS_Lemoore" title="NAS Lemoore" class="mw-redirect">NAS Lemoore</a>, California and <a href="/wiki/NAS_Whidbey_Island" title="NAS Whidbey Island" class="mw-redirect">NAS Whidbey Island</a>, Washington, while the carrier-based airborne early warning aircraft community and major air test activities are located at <a href="/wiki/NAS_Point_Mugu" title="NAS Point Mugu" class="mw-redirect">NAS Point Mugu</a>, California. The naval presence in Hawaii is centered on <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor Naval Base</a>, which hosts the headquarters of the Pacific Fleet and many of its subordinate commands.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_territories">United States territories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: United States territories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_030527-N-0000X-001_The_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_(CVN_70)_pier_side_in_Apra_Harbor,_Guam.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/US_Navy_030527-N-0000X-001_The_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29_pier_side_in_Apra_Harbor%2C_Guam.jpg/220px-US_Navy_030527-N-0000X-001_The_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29_pier_side_in_Apra_Harbor%2C_Guam.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/US_Navy_030527-N-0000X-001_The_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29_pier_side_in_Apra_Harbor%2C_Guam.jpg/330px-US_Navy_030527-N-0000X-001_The_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29_pier_side_in_Apra_Harbor%2C_Guam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/US_Navy_030527-N-0000X-001_The_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29_pier_side_in_Apra_Harbor%2C_Guam.jpg/440px-US_Navy_030527-N-0000X-001_The_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29_pier_side_in_Apra_Harbor%2C_Guam.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_030527-N-0000X-001_The_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_(CVN_70)_pier_side_in_Apra_Harbor,_Guam.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/USS_Carl_Vinson_(CVN-70)" title="USS Carl Vinson (CVN-70)" class="mw-redirect">USS&#160;<i>Carl Vinson</i>&#160;(CVN-70)</a> pier side in <a href="/wiki/Apra_Harbor" title="Apra Harbor">Apra Harbor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Guam#Villages_and_military_bases" title="Guam">Guam</a>, an island strategically located in the Western Pacific Ocean, maintains a sizable U.S. Navy presence, including <a href="/wiki/Naval_Base_Guam" title="Naval Base Guam">Naval Base Guam</a>. The westernmost U.S. territory, it contains a natural deep water harbor capable of harboring aircraft carriers in emergencies.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Naval_air_station" title="Naval air station">naval air station</a> was deactivated<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> in 1995 and its flight activities transferred to nearby <a href="/wiki/Andersen_Air_Force_Base" title="Andersen Air Force Base">Andersen Air Force Base</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> in the Caribbean formerly housed <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Roads_Naval_Station" title="Roosevelt Roads Naval Station">Roosevelt Roads Naval Station</a>, which was shut down in 2004 shortly after the controversial closure of the live ordnance training area on nearby <a href="/wiki/Vieques,_Puerto_Rico" title="Vieques, Puerto Rico">Vieques Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-status_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-status-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_countries">Foreign countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Foreign countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The largest overseas base is in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Fleet_Activities_Yokosuka" title="United States Fleet Activities Yokosuka">Yokosuka</a>, Japan,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> which serves as the home port for the navy's largest forward-deployed fleet and is a significant base of operations in the Western Pacific.</p>
<p>European operations revolve around facilities in Italy (<a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Sigonella" title="Naval Air Station Sigonella">Sigonella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naval_Computer_and_Telecommunications_Station_Naples,_Italy" title="Naval Computer and Telecommunications Station Naples, Italy">Naples</a>), <a href="/wiki/Naval_Station_Rota,_Spain" title="Naval Station Rota, Spain">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> with Naples as the homeport for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Sixth_Fleet" title="United States Sixth Fleet">Sixth Fleet</a> and Command Naval Region Europe (CNRE), which are based in <a href="/wiki/Gaeta" title="Gaeta">Gaeta</a>.</p>
<p>In the Middle East, naval facilities are located almost exclusively in countries bordering the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Manama,_Bahrain" title="Manama, Bahrain" class="mw-redirect">Manama, Bahrain</a> serving as the headquarters of <a href="/wiki/U.S._Naval_Forces_Central_Command" title="U.S. Naval Forces Central Command" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Naval Forces Central Command</a> and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Fifth_Fleet" title="U.S. Fifth Fleet" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Fifth Fleet</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Guantanamo_Bay_Naval_Base" title="Guantanamo Bay Naval Base">Guantánamo Bay</a> in Cuba is the oldest overseas facility and has become known in recent years as the location of a <a href="/wiki/Guantanamo_Bay_detention_camp" title="Guantanamo Bay detention camp">detention camp</a> for suspected <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a> operatives.<sup id="cite_ref-ForeignFacilities_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ForeignFacilities-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Equipment_of_the_United_States_Navy" title="Equipment of the United States Navy">Equipment of the United States Navy</a></div>
<p>As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the navy operates 283 ships, 3,659 aircraft, 50,000 non-combat vehicles and owns 75,200 buildings on 3,300,000 acres (13,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>). In addition, the Navy has 122 ships operated by the Military Sealift Command (MSC) crewed by a combination of civilian contractors and a small number of uniformed Naval personnel.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ships">Ships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Bataan_(LHD-5);10080504.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/USS_Bataan_%28LHD-5%29%3B10080504.jpg/220px-USS_Bataan_%28LHD-5%29%3B10080504.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/USS_Bataan_%28LHD-5%29%3B10080504.jpg/330px-USS_Bataan_%28LHD-5%29%3B10080504.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/USS_Bataan_%28LHD-5%29%3B10080504.jpg/440px-USS_Bataan_%28LHD-5%29%3B10080504.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1680" /></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_040730-N-1234E-002_PCU_Virginia_(SSN_774)_returns_to_the_General_Dynamics_Electric_Boat_shipyard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_Navy_040730-N-1234E-002_PCU_Virginia_%28SSN_774%29_returns_to_the_General_Dynamics_Electric_Boat_shipyard.jpg/220px-US_Navy_040730-N-1234E-002_PCU_Virginia_%28SSN_774%29_returns_to_the_General_Dynamics_Electric_Boat_shipyard.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_Navy_040730-N-1234E-002_PCU_Virginia_%28SSN_774%29_returns_to_the_General_Dynamics_Electric_Boat_shipyard.jpg/330px-US_Navy_040730-N-1234E-002_PCU_Virginia_%28SSN_774%29_returns_to_the_General_Dynamics_Electric_Boat_shipyard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_Navy_040730-N-1234E-002_PCU_Virginia_%28SSN_774%29_returns_to_the_General_Dynamics_Electric_Boat_shipyard.jpg/440px-US_Navy_040730-N-1234E-002_PCU_Virginia_%28SSN_774%29_returns_to_the_General_Dynamics_Electric_Boat_shipyard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_040730-N-1234E-002_PCU_Virginia_(SSN_774)_returns_to_the_General_Dynamics_Electric_Boat_shipyard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/USS_Virginia_(SSN-774)" title="USS Virginia (SSN-774)">USS <i>Virginia</i></a>, a <a href="/wiki/Virginia-class_submarine" title="Virginia-class submarine"><i>Virginia</i> -class</a> <a href="/wiki/Attack_submarine" title="Attack submarine">attack submarine</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The names of commissioned ships of the U.S. Navy are prefixed with the letters "USS", designating "United States Ship".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Non-commissioned, civilian-manned vessels of the navy have names that begin with "USNS", standing for "United States Naval Ship" The names of ships are officially selected by the secretary of the navy, often to honor important people or places.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, each ship is given a letter-based <a href="/wiki/Hull_classification_symbol" title="Hull classification symbol">hull classification symbol</a> (for example, CVN or DDG) to indicate the vessel's type and number. All ships in the navy inventory are placed in the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Vessel_Register" title="Naval Vessel Register">Naval Vessel Register</a>, which is part of "the Navy List" (required by article 29 of the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement" class="mw-redirect"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (July 2013)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:United_States_Navy#Dubious" title="Talk:United States Navy">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> The register tracks data such as the current status of a ship, the date of its commissioning, and the date of its decommissioning. Vessels that are removed from the register prior to disposal are said to be <i>stricken</i> from the register. The navy also maintains a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_reserve_fleets" title="United States Navy reserve fleets">reserve fleet</a> of inactive vessels that are maintained for reactivation in times of need.</p>
<p>The U.S. Navy was one of the first to install <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> aboard naval vessels;<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> today, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear energy</a> powers all active U.S. aircraft carriers and <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a>. In the case of the <a href="/wiki/Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Nimitz-class aircraft carrier"><i>Nimitz</i>-class</a> carrier, two <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_reactor" title="United States Naval reactor" class="mw-redirect">naval reactors</a> give the ship almost unlimited range and provide enough electrical energy to power a city of 100,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> The U.S. Navy previously operated nuclear-powered cruisers, but all have been decommissioned.</p>
<p>The U.S. Navy has identified a need for 313 combat ships, but under the current plans will only be able to afford 232 to 243.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> In March 2014, the Navy is considering counting deployable ships such as minesweepers, patrol craft, and hospital ships in the "battle fleet" in order to reach a count of 291,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> and also ships that have been put in "shrink wrap".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft_carriers">Aircraft carriers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Aircraft carriers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The navy had established a minimum requirement for 11 aircraft carriers, but dropped to 10 when <i>Enterprise</i> was retired in December 2012, before <i>Gerald R. Ford</i> enters service.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A carrier is typically deployed along with a host of additional vessels, forming a <a href="/wiki/Carrier_strike_group" title="Carrier strike group">carrier strike group</a>. The supporting ships, which usually include three or four <a href="/wiki/Aegis_combat_system" title="Aegis combat system" class="mw-redirect">Aegis</a>-equipped cruisers and destroyers, a frigate, and two attack submarines, are tasked with protecting the carrier from air, missile, sea, and undersea threats as well as providing additional strike capabilities themselves. Ready logistics support for the group is provided by a combined ammunition, oiler, and supply ship.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Nimitz-class aircraft carrier"><i>Nimitz</i>&#160;class</a> – 10 in commission</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gerald_R._Ford-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Gerald R. Ford-class aircraft carrier"><i>Gerald R. Ford</i>&#160;class</a> – 1 waiting commission,1 under construction, and 10 planned</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Amphibious_warfare_vessels">Amphibious warfare vessels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Amphibious warfare vessels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Amphibious_assault_ship" title="Amphibious assault ship">Amphibious assault ships</a> are the centerpieces of US amphibious warfare and fulfill the same power projection role as aircraft carriers except that their striking force centers on land forces instead of aircraft. They deliver, command, coordinate, and fully support all elements of a 2,200-strong <a href="/wiki/Marine_Expeditionary_Unit" title="Marine Expeditionary Unit" class="mw-redirect">Marine Expeditionary Unit</a> in an amphibious assault using both air and amphibious vehicles. Resembling small aircraft carriers, amphibious assault ships are capable of <a href="/wiki/V/STOL" title="V/STOL">V/STOL</a>, <a href="/wiki/STOVL" title="STOVL">STOVL</a>, <a href="/wiki/VTOL" title="VTOL">VTOL</a>, tiltrotor, and rotary wing aircraft operations. They also contain a <a href="/wiki/Well_deck" title="Well deck">well deck</a> to support the use of <a href="/wiki/Landing_Craft_Air_Cushion" title="Landing Craft Air Cushion">Landing Craft Air Cushion</a> (LCAC) and other amphibious assault watercraft. Recently, amphibious assault ships have begun to be deployed as the core of an <a href="/wiki/Expeditionary_strike_group" title="Expeditionary strike group" class="mw-redirect">expeditionary strike group</a>, which usually consists of an additional <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_transport_dock" title="Amphibious transport dock">amphibious transport dock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dock_landing_ship" title="Dock landing ship">dock landing ship</a> for amphibious warfare and an Aegis-equipped cruiser and destroyer, frigate, and attack submarine for group defense. Amphibious assault ships are typically named after World War II aircraft carriers.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wasp-class_amphibious_assault_ship" title="Wasp-class amphibious assault ship"><i>Wasp</i>&#160;class</a> – 8 in commission</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/America-class_amphibious_assault_ship" title="America-class amphibious assault ship"><i>America</i>&#160;class</a> – 1 in commission, 1 under construction, 9 more planned<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Amphibious transport docks are warships that embark, transport, and land Marines, supplies, and equipment in a supporting role during amphibious warfare missions. With a landing platform, amphibious transport docks also have the capability to serve as secondary aviation support for an expeditionary group. All amphibious transport docks can operate helicopters, LCACs, and other conventional amphibious vehicles while the newer <i>San Antonio</i> class of ships has been explicitly designed to operate all three elements of the Marines' "mobility triad": <a href="/wiki/Expeditionary_Fighting_Vehicle" title="Expeditionary Fighting Vehicle">Expeditionary Fighting Vehicles</a> (EFVs), the <a href="/wiki/V-22_Osprey" title="V-22 Osprey" class="mw-redirect">V-22 Osprey</a> tiltrotor aircraft, and LCACs. Amphibious transport docks are named for cities, except for <a href="/wiki/USS_Mesa_Verde_(LPD-19)" title="USS Mesa Verde (LPD-19)">USS&#160;<i>Mesa Verde</i>&#160;(LPD-19)</a>, named for <a href="/wiki/Mesa_Verde_National_Park" title="Mesa Verde National Park">Mesa Verde National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, and three <i>San Antonio</i> class ships named in memory of the <a href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" title="September 11, 2001 attacks" class="mw-redirect">September 11, 2001 attacks</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Austin-class_amphibious_transport_dock" title="Austin-class amphibious transport dock"><i>Austin</i>&#160;class</a> – 1 in commission for Laser weapon testbed</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/San_Antonio-class_amphibious_transport_dock" title="San Antonio-class amphibious transport dock"><i>San Antonio</i>&#160;class</a> – 9 in commission, 2 under construction</li>
</ul>
<p>The dock landing ship is a medium amphibious transport that is designed specifically to support and operate LCACs, though it is able to operate other amphibious assault vehicles in the United States inventory as well. Dock landing ships are normally deployed as a component of an expeditionary strike group's amphibious assault contingent, operating as a secondary launch platform for LCACs. All dock landing ships are named after locations in the United States.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Whidbey_Island-class_dock_landing_ship" title="Whidbey Island-class dock landing ship"><i>Whidbey Island</i>&#160;class</a> – 8 in commission</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harpers_Ferry-class_dock_landing_ship" title="Harpers Ferry-class dock landing ship"><i>Harpers Ferry</i>&#160;class</a> – 4 in commission</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_vessels">Surface vessels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Surface vessels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cruiser" title="Cruiser">Cruisers</a> are large surface combat vessels that conduct anti-air/anti-missile warfare, surface warfare, anti-submarine warfare, and strike operations independently or as members of a larger task force. Modern guided missile cruisers were developed out of a need to counter the <a href="/wiki/Anti-ship_missile" title="Anti-ship missile">anti-ship missile</a> threat facing the United States Navy. This led to the development of the <a href="/wiki/AN/SPY-1" title="AN/SPY-1">AN/SPY-1</a> phased array radar and the <a href="/wiki/RIM-67_Standard" title="RIM-67 Standard">Standard missile</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Aegis_combat_system" title="Aegis combat system" class="mw-redirect">Aegis combat system</a> coordinating the two. <a href="/wiki/Ticonderoga-class_cruiser" title="Ticonderoga-class cruiser"><i>Ticonderoga</i>-class</a> cruisers were the first to be equipped with Aegis and were put to use primarily as anti-air and anti-missile defense in a battle force protection role. Later developments of <a href="/wiki/Vertical_launch_system" title="Vertical launch system" class="mw-redirect">vertical launch systems</a> and the <a href="/wiki/BGM-109_Tomahawk" title="BGM-109 Tomahawk" class="mw-redirect">Tomahawk missile</a> gave cruisers additional long-range land and sea strike capability, making them capable of both offensive and defensive battle operations. All cruisers since CG-47 have been named for famous battles with <a href="/wiki/USS_Thomas_S._Gates_(CG-51)" title="USS Thomas S. Gates (CG-51)" class="mw-redirect">USS&#160;<i>Thomas S. Gates</i>&#160;(CG-51)</a> as the only exception. Previously, cruisers were either named for cities (until CG-12), former important navy figures (CG-15 to CG-35), or states (CGN-36 to CGN-41).</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ticonderoga-class_cruiser" title="Ticonderoga-class cruiser"><i>Ticonderoga</i>&#160;class</a> – 22 in commission</li>
</ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Destroyers" title="Destroyers" class="mw-redirect">Destroyers</a> are multi-mission medium surface ships capable of sustained performance in anti-air, anti-submarine, anti-ship, and offensive strike operations. Like cruisers, guided missile destroyers are primarily focused on surface strikes using <a href="/wiki/BGM-109_Tomahawk" title="BGM-109 Tomahawk" class="mw-redirect">Tomahawk missiles</a> and fleet defense through Aegis and the Standard missile. Destroyers additionally specialize in anti-submarine warfare and are equipped with <a href="/wiki/ASROC" title="ASROC" class="mw-redirect">VLA rockets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_SH-60_Seahawk" title="Sikorsky SH-60 Seahawk">LAMPS Mk III Sea Hawk</a> helicopters to deal with underwater threats. When deployed with a carrier strike group or expeditionary strike group, destroyers and their fellow Aegis-equipped cruisers are primarily tasked with defending the fleet while providing secondary strike capabilities. Destroyers have been named for important navy personnel and heroes since <a href="/wiki/USS_Bainbridge_(DD-1)" title="USS Bainbridge (DD-1)">USS&#160;<i>Bainbridge</i>&#160;(DD-1)</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arleigh_Burke-class_destroyer" title="Arleigh Burke-class destroyer"><i>Arleigh Burke</i>&#160;class</a> – 62 in commission, 6 under construction, 8 more planned<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zumwalt-class_destroyer" title="Zumwalt-class destroyer"><i>Zumwalt</i>&#160;class</a> – 1 to be in commission, 2 under construction</li>
</ul>
<p>Modern U.S. <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigates</a> mainly perform anti-submarine warfare for carrier strike groups and amphibious expeditionary groups and provide armed escort for supply convoys and merchant shipping. They are designed to protect friendly ships against hostile submarines in low to medium threat environments, using torpedoes and LAMPS helicopters. Independently, frigates are able to conduct counterdrug missions and other maritime interception operations. The U.S. Navy expects to retire and replace its current class of frigates by 2020 as the <a href="/wiki/Littoral_Combat_Ship" title="Littoral Combat Ship" class="mw-redirect">Littoral Combat Ships</a> are introduced into operation.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> As in the case of destroyers, frigates are named after naval heroes.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Hazard_Perry-class_frigate" title="Oliver Hazard Perry-class frigate"><i>Oliver Hazard Perry</i>&#160;class</a> – all decommissioned</li>
</ul>
<p>Littoral Combat Ships are split between two designs and are expected to 32 ships when the program is complete.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Freedom-class_littoral_combat_ship" title="Freedom-class littoral combat ship"><i>Freedom</i>&#160;class</a> – 2 in commission, 2 under construction, 12 more planned</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Independence-class_littoral_combat_ship" title="Independence-class littoral combat ship"><i>Independence</i>&#160;class</a> – 2 in commission, 2 under construction, 12 more planned</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition, <a href="/wiki/USS_Constitution" title="USS Constitution">USS&#160;<i>Constitution</i></a>, commissioned in 1797 and one of the original six frigates of the United States Navy, remains in commission at the Charlestown Navy Yard in Boston. She serves as a tribute to the heritage of the Navy, and occasionally sails for commemorative events such as <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">Independence Day</a> and various victories during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>. <i>Constitution</i> is currently the oldest commissioned warship afloat. <a href="/wiki/HMS_Victory" title="HMS Victory">HMS&#160;<i>Victory</i></a> is older, and in commission, but is in permanent drydock.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Submarines">Submarines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Submarines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Submarines_in_the_United_States_Navy" title="Submarines in the United States Navy">Submarines in the United States Navy</a></div>
<p>All current and planned U.S. Navy submarines are nuclear-powered, as only nuclear propulsion allows for the combination of stealth and long duration, high-speed sustained underwater movement that makes modern nuclear submarines so vital to a modern blue-water navy. The U.S. Navy operates three types: <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile_submarine" title="Ballistic missile submarine">ballistic missile submarines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guided_missile_submarine" title="Guided missile submarine" class="mw-redirect">guided missile submarines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Attack_submarine" title="Attack submarine">attack submarines</a>. U.S. Navy (nuclear) ballistic missile submarines carry the stealthiest leg of the U.S. strategic triad (the other legs are the land-based U.S. strategic missile force and the air-based U.S. strategic bomber force). These submarines have only one mission: to carry and, if called upon, to launch the <a href="/wiki/Trident_nuclear_missile" title="Trident nuclear missile" class="mw-redirect">Trident nuclear missile</a>. The primary missions of attack and guided missile submarines in the U.S. Navy are peacetime engagement, surveillance and intelligence, special operations, precision strikes, and control of the seas.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> To these, attack submarines also add the battlegroup operations mission. Attack and guided missile submarines have several tactical missions, including sinking ships and other subs, launching <a href="/wiki/Cruise_missile" title="Cruise missile">cruise missiles</a>, gathering intelligence, and assisting in special operations.</p>
<p>As with other classes of naval vessels, most U.S. submarines (or "boats") are named according to specific conventions. The boats of the current U.S. ballistic missile submarine class, <a href="/wiki/Ohio-class_submarine" title="Ohio-class submarine"><i>Ohio</i>-class</a>, are named after U.S. states. As the four current U.S. guided missile submarines are converted <i>Ohio</i>-class boats, they have retained their U.S. state names. The members of the oldest currently-commissioned attack submarine class, the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles-class_submarine" title="Los Angeles-class submarine"><i>Los Angeles</i>&#160;class</a>, are typically named for cities. The follow-on <a href="/wiki/Seawolf-class_submarine" title="Seawolf-class submarine"><i>Seawolf</i>-class</a>' three submarines -- <i>Seawolf</i>, <i>Connecticut</i> and <i>Jimmy Carter</i>—share no consistent naming scheme. With the current <a href="/wiki/Virginia-class_submarine" title="Virginia-class submarine"><i>Virginia</i>-class</a> class attack submarines, the U.S. Navy has extended the <i>Ohio</i> class' state-based naming scheme to these submarines. Attack submarines prior to the <i>Los Angeles</i> class were named for denizens of the deep, while pre-<i>Ohio</i>-class ballistic missile submarines were named for famous Americans and foreigners with notable connections to the United States.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ohio-class_submarine" title="Ohio-class submarine"><i>Ohio</i>-class</a> ballistic missile submarines – 14 in commission</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ohio-class_submarine" title="Ohio-class submarine"><i>Ohio</i>-class</a> guided missile submarines – 4 in commission</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles-class_submarine" title="Los Angeles-class submarine"><i>Los Angeles</i>-class</a> attack submarines – 40 in commission</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Seawolf-class_submarine" title="Seawolf-class submarine"><i>Seawolf</i>-class</a> attack submarines – 3 in commission</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Virginia-class_submarine" title="Virginia-class submarine"><i>Virginia</i>-class</a> attack submarines – 12 in commission, 3 under construction, at least 33 more planned (Block I-VII)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft">Aircraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Aircraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Four_Super_Hornets.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Four_Super_Hornets.jpg/220px-Four_Super_Hornets.jpg" width="220" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Four_Super_Hornets.jpg/330px-Four_Super_Hornets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Four_Super_Hornets.jpg/440px-Four_Super_Hornets.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1960" data-file-height="3008" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Four_Super_Hornets.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Four Navy <a href="/wiki/F/A-18E/F_Super_Hornet" title="F/A-18E/F Super Hornet" class="mw-redirect">F/A-18F Super Hornets</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_naval_aircraft" title="List of United States naval aircraft">List of United States naval aircraft</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_military_aircraft_of_the_United_States_(naval)" title="List of military aircraft of the United States (naval)">List of military aircraft of the United States (naval)</a></div>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Navy_aircraft_squadrons" title="List of United States Navy aircraft squadrons">List of United States Navy aircraft squadrons</a></div>
<p>Carrier-based aircraft are able to strike air, sea, and land targets far from a carrier strike group while protecting friendly forces from enemy aircraft, ships, and submarines. In peacetime, aircraft's ability to project the threat of sustained attack from a mobile platform on the seas gives United States leaders significant diplomatic and crisis-management options. Aircraft additionally provide logistics support to maintain the navy's readiness and, through helicopters, supply platforms with which to conduct <a href="/wiki/Search_and_rescue" title="Search and rescue">search and rescue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Special_operations" title="Special operations">special operations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-submarine_warfare" title="Anti-submarine warfare">anti-submarine warfare</a> (ASW), and <a href="/wiki/Anti-surface_warfare" title="Anti-surface warfare">anti-surface warfare</a> (ASuW).</p>
<p>The U.S. Navy began to research the use of aircraft at sea in the 1910s, with Lieutenant Theodore G. "Spuds" Ellyson becoming the first naval aviator on 28 January 1911, and commissioned its first aircraft carrier, <a href="/wiki/USS_Langley_(CV-1)" title="USS Langley (CV-1)">USS&#160;<i>Langley</i>&#160;(CV-1)</a>, in 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> United States naval aviation fully came of age in World War II, when it became clear following the <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">Attack on Pearl Harbor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Coral_Sea" title="Battle of the Coral Sea">Battle of the Coral Sea</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Midway" title="Battle of Midway">Battle of Midway</a> that aircraft carriers and the planes that they carried had replaced the battleship as the greatest weapon on the seas. Leading navy aircraft in World War II included the <a href="/wiki/Grumman_F4F_Wildcat" title="Grumman F4F Wildcat">Grumman F4F Wildcat</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Grumman_F6F_Hellcat" title="Grumman F6F Hellcat">Grumman F6F Hellcat</a>, the Chance <a href="/wiki/Vought_F4U_Corsair" title="Vought F4U Corsair">Vought F4U Corsair</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Douglas_SBD_Dauntless" title="Douglas SBD Dauntless">Douglas SBD Dauntless</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Grumman_TBF_Avenger" title="Grumman TBF Avenger">Grumman TBF Avenger</a>. Navy aircraft also played a significant role in conflicts during the following Cold War years, with the <a href="/wiki/F-4_Phantom_II" title="F-4 Phantom II" class="mw-redirect">F-4 Phantom II</a> and the <a href="/wiki/F-14_Tomcat" title="F-14 Tomcat" class="mw-redirect">F-14 Tomcat</a> becoming military icons of the era. The navy's current primary fighter and attack airplanes are the multi-mission <a href="/wiki/F/A-18_Hornet" title="F/A-18 Hornet" class="mw-redirect">F/A-18C/D Hornet</a> and its newer cousin, the <a href="/wiki/F/A-18E/F_Super_Hornet" title="F/A-18E/F Super Hornet" class="mw-redirect">F/A-18E/F Super Hornet</a>. The <a href="/wiki/F-35_Lightning_II" title="F-35 Lightning II" class="mw-redirect">F-35 Lightning II</a> is presently under development and was scheduled to replace the C and D versions of the Hornet beginning in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Initial operational capability of the F-35C is now expected to be February 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> The Navy is also looking to eventually replace its F/A-18E/F Super Hornets with the <a href="/wiki/F/A-XX" title="F/A-XX" class="mw-redirect">F/A-XX</a> program.</p>
<p>The Aircraft Investment Plan sees naval aviation growing from 30 percent of current aviation forces to half of all procurement funding over the next three decades.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weapons">Weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Navy_weapons" title="List of United States Navy weapons">List of United States Navy weapons</a></div>
<p>Current U.S. Navy shipboard weapons systems are almost entirely focused on missiles, both as a weapon and as a threat. In an offensive role, missiles are intended to strike targets at long distances with accuracy and precision. Because they are unmanned weapons, missiles allow for attacks on heavily defended targets without risk to human pilots. Land strikes are the domain of the <a href="/wiki/BGM-109_Tomahawk" title="BGM-109 Tomahawk" class="mw-redirect">BGM-109 Tomahawk</a>, which was first deployed in the 1980s and is continually being updated to increase its capabilities. For anti-ship strikes, the navy's dedicated missile is the <a href="/wiki/Harpoon_Missile" title="Harpoon Missile" class="mw-redirect">Harpoon Missile</a>. To defend against enemy missile attack, the navy operates a number of systems that are all coordinated by the Aegis combat system. Medium-long range defense is provided by the <a href="/wiki/RIM-67_Standard" title="RIM-67 Standard">Standard Missile 2</a>, which has been deployed since the 1980s. The Standard missile doubles as the primary shipboard anti-aircraft weapon and is undergoing development for use in theater ballistic missile defense. Short range defense against missiles is provided by the <a href="/wiki/Phalanx_CIWS" title="Phalanx CIWS">Phalanx CIWS</a> and the more recently developed <a href="/wiki/ESSM" title="ESSM" class="mw-redirect">RIM-162 Evolved Sea Sparrow Missile</a>. In addition to missiles, the navy employs <a href="/wiki/Mark_46_torpedo" title="Mark 46 torpedo">Mark 46</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_50_torpedo" title="Mark 50 torpedo">Mark 50 torpedoes</a> and various types of naval mines.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_050112-N-5345W-074_Aviation_Ordnancemen_prepare_to_load_500-pound_laser_guided_bombs_(GBU-12)_onto_weapon_pylons_under_an_F-14B_Tomcat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/US_Navy_050112-N-5345W-074_Aviation_Ordnancemen_prepare_to_load_500-pound_laser_guided_bombs_%28GBU-12%29_onto_weapon_pylons_under_an_F-14B_Tomcat.jpg/220px-US_Navy_050112-N-5345W-074_Aviation_Ordnancemen_prepare_to_load_500-pound_laser_guided_bombs_%28GBU-12%29_onto_weapon_pylons_under_an_F-14B_Tomcat.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/US_Navy_050112-N-5345W-074_Aviation_Ordnancemen_prepare_to_load_500-pound_laser_guided_bombs_%28GBU-12%29_onto_weapon_pylons_under_an_F-14B_Tomcat.jpg/330px-US_Navy_050112-N-5345W-074_Aviation_Ordnancemen_prepare_to_load_500-pound_laser_guided_bombs_%28GBU-12%29_onto_weapon_pylons_under_an_F-14B_Tomcat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/US_Navy_050112-N-5345W-074_Aviation_Ordnancemen_prepare_to_load_500-pound_laser_guided_bombs_%28GBU-12%29_onto_weapon_pylons_under_an_F-14B_Tomcat.jpg/440px-US_Navy_050112-N-5345W-074_Aviation_Ordnancemen_prepare_to_load_500-pound_laser_guided_bombs_%28GBU-12%29_onto_weapon_pylons_under_an_F-14B_Tomcat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_050112-N-5345W-074_Aviation_Ordnancemen_prepare_to_load_500-pound_laser_guided_bombs_(GBU-12)_onto_weapon_pylons_under_an_F-14B_Tomcat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aviation Ordnancemen loading <a href="/wiki/GBU-12_Paveway_II" title="GBU-12 Paveway II">GBU-12</a> bombs in 2005.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Naval fixed-wing aircraft employ much of the same weapons as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> for both air-to-air and air-to-surface combat. Air engagements are handled by the heat-seeking <a href="/wiki/AIM-9_Sidewinder" title="AIM-9 Sidewinder">Sidewinder</a> and the radar guided <a href="/wiki/AIM-120_AMRAAM" title="AIM-120 AMRAAM">AMRAAM</a> missiles along with the <a href="/wiki/M61_Vulcan" title="M61 Vulcan">M61 Vulcan</a> cannon for close range dogfighting. For surface strikes, navy aircraft utilize a combination of missiles, smart bombs, and dumb bombs. On the list of available missiles are the <a href="/wiki/AGM-65_Maverick" title="AGM-65 Maverick">Maverick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Standoff_Land_Attack_Missile" title="Standoff Land Attack Missile" class="mw-redirect">SLAM-ER</a> and <a href="/wiki/AGM-154_Joint_Standoff_Weapon" title="AGM-154 Joint Standoff Weapon">JSOW</a>. Smart bombs include the GPS-guided <a href="/wiki/JDAM" title="JDAM" class="mw-redirect">JDAM</a> and the laser-guided <a href="/wiki/Paveway" title="Paveway">Paveway</a> series. Unguided munitions such as dumb bombs and <a href="/wiki/Cluster_bomb" title="Cluster bomb" class="mw-redirect">cluster bombs</a> make up the rest of the weapons deployed by fixed-wing aircraft.</p>
<p>Rotary aircraft weapons are focused on anti-submarine warfare (ASW) and light to medium surface engagements. To combat submarines, helicopters use Mark 46 and Mark 50 torpedoes. Against small watercraft, they utilize <a href="/wiki/AGM-114_Hellfire" title="AGM-114 Hellfire">Hellfire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Penguin_missile" title="Penguin missile" class="mw-redirect">Penguin</a> air to surface missiles. Helicopters also employ various types of mounted anti-personnel machine guns, including the <a href="/wiki/M60_machine_gun" title="M60 machine gun">M60</a>, <a href="/wiki/M240_machine_gun" title="M240 machine gun">M240</a>, <a href="/wiki/GAU-16" title="GAU-16" class="mw-redirect">GAU-16/A</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Minigun" title="Minigun">GAU-17/A</a>.</p>
<p>Nuclear weapons in the U.S. Navy arsenal are deployed through ballistic missile submarines and aircraft. The <i>Ohio</i>-class submarine carries the latest iteration of the <a href="/wiki/Trident_missile" title="Trident missile" class="mw-redirect">Trident missile</a>, a three-stage, <a href="/wiki/Submarine-launched_ballistic_missile" title="Submarine-launched ballistic missile">submarine-launched ballistic missile</a> (SLBM) with <a href="/wiki/MIRV" title="MIRV" class="mw-redirect">MIRV</a> capability; the current Trident II (D5) version is expected to be in service past 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> The navy's other nuclear weapon is the air-deployed <a href="/wiki/B61_nuclear_bomb" title="B61 nuclear bomb">B61 nuclear bomb</a>. The B61 is a thermonuclear device that can be dropped by strike aircraft such as the F/A-18 Hornet and Super Hornet at high speed from a large range of altitudes. It can be released through free-fall or parachute and can be set to detonate in the air or on the ground.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_jack">Naval jack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Naval jack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Naval_Jack_of_the_United_States.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Naval_Jack_of_the_United_States.svg/100px-Naval_Jack_of_the_United_States.svg.png" width="100" height="53" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Naval_Jack_of_the_United_States.svg/150px-Naval_Jack_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Naval_Jack_of_the_United_States.svg/200px-Naval_Jack_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1330" data-file-height="700" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Naval_Jack_of_the_United_States.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. Naval Jack</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Naval_Jack.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/US_Naval_Jack.svg/100px-US_Naval_Jack.svg.png" width="100" height="71" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/US_Naval_Jack.svg/150px-US_Naval_Jack.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/US_Naval_Jack.svg/200px-US_Naval_Jack.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1482" data-file-height="1050" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Naval_Jack.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/File:FIAV_historical.svg" class="image"><img alt="FIAV historical.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/FIAV_historical.svg/23px-FIAV_historical.svg.png" width="23" height="15" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/FIAV_historical.svg/35px-FIAV_historical.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/FIAV_historical.svg/46px-FIAV_historical.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="230" data-file-height="150" /></a> <a href="/wiki/File:FIAV_000001.svg" class="image"><img alt="FIAV 000001.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/FIAV_000001.svg/23px-FIAV_000001.svg.png" width="23" height="15" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/FIAV_000001.svg/35px-FIAV_000001.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/FIAV_000001.svg/46px-FIAV_000001.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="210" /></a> Former U.S. Naval Jack</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The current <a href="/wiki/Maritime_flags#Jacks" title="Maritime flags" class="mw-redirect">naval jack</a> of the United States is the <a href="/wiki/First_Navy_Jack" title="First Navy Jack">First Navy Jack</a>, traditionally regarded as having been used during the American Revolutionary War. On 31 May 2002, <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Secretary of the Navy" class="mw-redirect">Secretary of the Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Gordon_R._England" title="Gordon R. England">Gordon R. England</a> directed all U.S. naval ships to fly the First Navy Jack for the duration of the "<a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a>". Many ships chose to shift colors later that year on the first anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" title="September 11, 2001 attacks" class="mw-redirect">September 11, 2001 attacks</a>. The previous naval jack was a blue field with 50 white stars, identical to the canton of the <a href="/wiki/Ensign" title="Ensign">ensign</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_States" title="Flag of the United States">flag of the United States</a>) both in appearance and size, and remains in use with vessels of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Coast_Guard" title="U.S. Coast Guard" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Coast Guard</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a>. A jack of similar design was used in 1794, though with 13 stars arranged in a 3–2–3–2–3 pattern. When a ship is moored or anchored, the jack is flown from the <a href="/wiki/Bow_(ship)" title="Bow (ship)">bow</a> of the ship while the ensign is flown from the <a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">stern</a>. When underway, the ensign is raised on the mainmast. The First Naval Jack, however, has always been flown on the oldest ship in the active American fleet, which is currently <a href="/wiki/USS_Blue_Ridge_(LCC-19)" title="USS Blue Ridge (LCC-19)">USS&#160;<i>Blue Ridge</i>&#160;(LCC-19)</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_sailors">Notable sailors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Notable sailors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Navy_people" title="List of United States Navy people">List of United States Navy people</a></div>
<p>Many past and present United States historical figures have served in the navy. Notable officers include <a href="/wiki/John_Paul_Jones" title="John Paul Jones">John Paul Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Barry_(naval_officer)" title="John Barry (naval officer)">John Barry</a> (<a href="/wiki/Continental_Navy" title="Continental Navy">Continental Navy</a> officer and first flag officer of the United States Navy),<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Preble" title="Edward Preble">Edward Preble</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Lawrence" title="James Lawrence">James Lawrence</a> (whose <a href="/wiki/Last_words" title="Last words">last words</a> "don't give up the ship" are memorialized in <a href="/wiki/Bancroft_Hall" title="Bancroft Hall">Bancroft Hall</a> at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">United States Naval Academy</a>), <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Decatur" title="Stephen Decatur">Stephen Decatur, Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Farragut" title="David Farragut">David Farragut</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Dixon_Porter" title="David Dixon Porter">David Dixon Porter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Hazard_Perry" title="Oliver Hazard Perry">Oliver Hazard Perry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Perry_(naval_officer)" title="Matthew Perry (naval officer)" class="mw-redirect">Commodore Matthew Perry</a> (whose <a href="/wiki/Black_Ships" title="Black Ships">Black Ships</a> forced the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Kanagawa" title="Convention of Kanagawa">opening of Japan</a>), <a href="/wiki/George_Dewey" title="George Dewey">George Dewey</a> (the only person in the history of the United States to have attained the rank of Admiral of the Navy), and the officers who attained the rank of Fleet Admiral during World War II: <a href="/wiki/William_D._Leahy" title="William D. Leahy">William D. Leahy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_J._King" title="Ernest J. King" class="mw-redirect">Ernest J. King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chester_W._Nimitz" title="Chester W. Nimitz">Chester W. Nimitz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_F._Halsey,_Jr." title="William F. Halsey, Jr." class="mw-redirect">William F. Halsey, Jr.</a>.</p>
<p>The first American <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">president</a> who served in the navy was <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> (who commanded the famous <i><a href="/wiki/Motor_Torpedo_Boat_PT-109" title="Motor Torpedo Boat PT-109">PT-109</a></i>). Others included <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>. Both <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> were the <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Assistant Secretary of the Navy">assistant secretary of the navy</a> prior to their presidencies. Many members of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> served in the navy, notably <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senator" title="United States Senator" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Senators</a> <a href="/wiki/Bob_Kerrey" title="Bob Kerrey">Bob Kerrey</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a>. Other notable former members of the U.S. Navy include astronauts, entertainers, authors and professional athletes.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
